There's an interesting duality between American and Swiss gun ownership/use, two very different motives for allowing it for the citizenry which translates to familiar but also very different ways of handling firearm ownership in either country. This might not fly in the US but rules on these things varies greatly from state to state, city to city, or even sometimes range to range. Many things are the same though, for example in Idaho ranges firearms are to be always unloaded except when at the firing line, firearms are always to be pointed down range, etc. I think the closest example I can think of  was an anecdote where a road got too close to the range for comfort and they had to create a really thick reinforced embankment to make it impossible for bullets to get through, they might have even buried steel plates in the embankment to leave no doubt. they still had to have someone come and verify though that there were no angles where someone could get hit. Gun ranges tend to get a lot of scrutiny so they tend to actually be fairly big on safety and firearm education.

Just some minor differences between Switzerland and the US that makes the two uncomparable:  - Every single person has tactical training that goes beyond firearms into hand-to-hand and psychological training. - Every single person passes a psychological and physical evaluation. - Every single gun owner has a license that has to be renewed. - Every single firearm is registered. - Most firearms are rifles, not pistols. - Most firearms are stored by the government, not the gun owner, and must be checked out before use, then checked back in after use. - Licenses can be revoked at any time, and firearms can be confiscated at any time. - Guns are not treated as self-defense weapons but as highly controlled sports utility or national defense weapons for war.

Switzerland's high rate of gun ownership is fueled mostly by the fact that the country has mandatory military service, and most former soldiers keep their service rifle or pistol. They also don't issue rifles to people deemed mentally unfit, or who have criminal records.  In America on the other hand, someone can be deemed unfit for gun ownership, but still allowed to buy one because their state doesn't permit background checks that would flag these problems. And that's is in states with supposedly tough gun laws.

I've been at a gun range here in the US where a teenager who had come with two adults turned around (facing the seats) with a loaded gun in hand. The rangemaster walked over and told the adults they were being ejected, and the teen was banned for a year. They protested and he told them "You're responsible for him, he could have killed someone, and you weren't paying enough attention to even notice - you're lucky he's not dead." Mind you, the kid was still holding the gun. The adult scoffed and asked why (about the luck in the kid not getting killed) and the rangemaster noted four people in adjacent stalls with weapons in hand, who had shifted their stances to face the confrontation (though still pointing their guns downrange, something they could remedy in a blink). The rest of the range (25 stalls) had stopped shooting, and everyone was shifting around to get out of the potential lines of fire. That's the thing about a bunch of trained shooters, nobody had to organize it, nobody had to tell anyone else what to do, they just did it.  When the two adults realized how much the situation had already escalated, they backed down and packed their things. The kid apologized, not to anyone in particular, but said he didn't mean to point it at anyone and he didn't mean anything by it. One of the guys who had been at the ready said, "We know - that's why you're still alive."

I live about 20 miles from a shooting range that shoots over a valley with a river running through it. The river is a very popular spot for swimming, boating and fishing- while enjoying the river you will hear gunshots and periodically bullets hit a cliff on across the river from the range. Nobody has ever been hurt. The cliff side where the bullets hit and the surrounding area are inaccessible, so there is no chance of someone wondering into the path of a bullet.   When Europeans travel to our area I always take them to this river so they can listen to gunfire and watch how causally Americans go about their recreation. This is usually a huge culture shock for most Europeans I have met.

Fun fact, fully automatic rifles are a class 4 firearm in Switzerland. This means it is a forbidden weapon. However as a civilian you can do some simple paperwork at the police station, telling that you would like to own class 4 firearms. The police will check if you have a criminal history, and if you don't you will get a permit for class 4. So basicly any civilian can own machineguns. And not just machineguns, also suppressors, very short barels, folding stocks, etc. You can own the most modern submachineguns in Switzerland and there are almost no incidents with firearms. Big contrast with america, where it is nearly imposible to own modern submachineguns and there are shootings every week.

Just in case anybody else likes statistics too. In comparison, according to a wikipedia entry the US has, more or less, 393.347.000 registered firearms [on 325 million inhabitants in 2017 so 1 gun per 0.8 citizen] and 39.707 firearm related deaths per year (cdc.gov). So every 1 in every 991th firearm will cause a death. Using this man's statistic of 2,4 million firearms [on 8,4 million inhabitants in 2016, 1 gun per 3,5 people] (which is an estimate, because firearms registration changed in 2008 when Switzerland joined Shengen) and an average of 2,64 [3] gun related deaths per year 1 in every 800.000 will cause a death in Switzerland.

Its the same reason Category R(estricted) licenses are so hard to get in Australia. The US Right uses Australia as an example of a country which had Gun freedom, revoked by the State, and all our guns taken away. Not true. We did have, gun laws similar the US, in most states, and in some, like Tasmania, possibly even more liberal. We also had around two mass shootings a year, give or take. Eventually we had a massacre in the Tasmanian town of Port Arthur, whereby Tasmania's liberal gun laws allowed a nutcase to get ahold of an assault rifle, and kill something like 36 people, and injure scores more. It was the worst active shooter incident in history, until Anders Brevik's attacks in 2011 in Norway.   In response the government implemented gun controls at the federal level, and passed laws to allow the States to bring all their state laws in to line with them, without having conflicts between the Federal and State governments. As a result, guns became incredibly hard to get, compared to what they had been. Still, it is easier than in many countries..  More, people seem to think this means we cant have guns, which is wrong. However, now, you need to have reasons, and in many instances before 1996, just wanting to own one, was reason enough, you got an easily procured permit, and went and got one, including pistols and automatic weapons in some places.   Most places scrapped access to Automatic weapons, but a couple of States did not. More, there is recourse for dispensation to have the license which lets you hold them, recognised by exemption in States which do not have the protocols to gain such a license in State.   So American gun nuts talk about how Australians cant own this or that gun now, thanks to the Right's media there using us as an example of 'State tyranny" but in actuality, since they have had a federal automatic weapons ban for decades in the US, we can actually own more than most US citizens can.   You can own an Assault weapon here.... if you go to the right State, and live, for the time you need to get it, and then show you meet the criteria.... that criteria is not only holding every increasingly harder to get lower category licenses, but meeting a valid reason threshold. Namely, for a Cat R license, being the curator or owner, or have intention to gather and be, a collection for public display and demonstration... as in a Museum collection.... or a specialist close protection and security contractor with personnel vetted, trained, and authorised to do such work, as in body guards who need to be armed, and may need have a sub-machine gun, or machine pistol, for the work, or be a anthorised trainer, with factory certified expertise in the wepaons you seek to hold, and be professionally engaged as such, in training police, military and private security personnel in the use and maintenance of such weapons.   This of course, requires whole crap ton of dedication and training, all kinds of paper work, and takes literally decades to achieve. Do so, and then you might be able to have your Cat R gun license and buy a machine gun, even in poster child of gun control Australia.....  .....because IF you DO go to all that trouble, to actually do everything you have to do, and jump through all the hoops you need to jump through, get all your clearances and lesser licenses, even relocate to live a new place for a time, and then meet the exceptionally high threshold.... you are probably the least likely person on earth to ever actually USE your weapons, at least outside those professional parameters. You might conduct a demonstration, you might train people on them, you may even have to fire it in a professional capacity as someone working say close protection work... but not for the sake of just enjoying the shooting of it. You went to a lot of trouble to get it, so if you have been deemed a 'fit and proper person' at every previous level of doing what you need to, to such a license, the powers that be figure you are highly unlikely to ever do something stupid once you get one.   If you were, you would be unable to demonstrate the ability to do everything you needed to do to get there.   It's a tried and true way of stopping morons having guns.... just make it so you have a lot of training, and follow a lot of rules, and file a lot of papers, and spend a lot of time, getting them. Psychos normally cant hack it, so there is less likelihood, be it Switzerland or Australia or any other places with such, any actually get a gun.

We do overhead fire on US military ranges too. In fact we do it in situations where the people whose heads are being fired over are very much visible, and even maneuvering, as long as the weapons being used are on properly set up tripods with proper limits set and we have proper points pre-planned for cut-fire on the maneuvering units.  But one of the things we have to do when setting up and planning one of those ranges is account for ricochet points between the shooter and the friendlies we are firing overhead of. Bullets need a much wider 45 degree fan after a ricochet point, meaning you have to account for a much wider area of possible impacts from the point of possible ricochet.  The top of that wall is a ricochet point. The area of road off to the right of the target points extends off to ranges well within the drop zone of ricochet rounds while being well within that 45 degree fan. So...I personally find the range a bit sketchy. Not actually because of the portion that travels under the intended bullet path, but due to the part that extends up and to the right of the target areas when accounting for the top of that wall as a ricochet point.  That said I'm sure the range is closely monitored and anyone that end up hitting the wall isn't going to be a member of that range for very long.

As nearly every village in Switzerland has such 300m shooting ranges (also as a part of the militia military system) there are many shooting over or along roads. The one in my village is exactly of this latter kind. It only has side baffles in the shooting stand itself but not along the 300m stretch to the targets. Which means the bang of the shot reaches you nearly head on, what makes a quite scary experience if you cycle along that road while they fire. Yet, it is perfectly safe. Another crazy thing is that in the Jungsch√ºtzen program (a pre-military training program from the age of 15-20) you are issued an assault rifle like the one shown here (SIG 550) starting from the age of 15 and you take your rifle home. Since I was in that program 35 years ago I never heard that there were any incidents with those rifles although thousands of kids participate in that program every year.

There are some excellent and informed explanations about gun ownership and why it's not a problem in Switzerland here but ultimately it boils down to one simple thing- mindset. In Switzerland, unlike some other countries, owning, carrying and using a weapon is correctly regarded as a privilege, not a right.   If something is a privilege, it has been earned, it will be cherished. It wouldn't matter if the weapon was a revolver, automatic rifle or Surface to Air Missile, if you recognise the privilege of being armed, you will equally respect the weapon and the damage it could do if not used properly

Since we have mandatory service for all men all of them get a rifle. You keep the rifle at home but you have to store different parts of the gun at different locations. What you don't usually have at home is munition for it. Also it is illegal to carry it if you're off duty. While we have high gun ownership (army rifle) the amount of civilians walking around with loaded guns is very low. If you're a sports shooter you leave the gun at the shooting range usually. Your observation about people willing to be trained and licensed not being the kind of folks who commit firearms accidents is why in the US firearm concealed carry permit holders not only have a lower incidence of firearms and criminal behavior than the public at large but lower than police officers. In my state, the folks willing to go down to the sheriff's office every few years to keep a permit current are not the folks who commit crimes. For a while I lived in very rural county in the US. We had a county built but volunteered maintained shooting range. Not only did we not have paid range officers the range use was on the honor system. In the 15ish years I used that range the number people shot or property damaged was zero. In fact because the range was technically a park you would have deer walk across it. They had learned they were safe.
Tom, I'd reccommend a look into Target Rifle shooting - it's big in the UK so not much travellling. It's a very technical Commonwealth Games sport (also Olympic at short range) with a surprisingly physical stance, if you take it seriously enough of course. When outdoors, we typically shoot between 300-1000 yards with iron sights (not telescopic). The national shooting centre at Bisley in Surrey is the range used for most National Open competitions. As a bonus (with reference to this video), the 1000 yard firing points are elevated about 8-10 ft off the ground; we regularly have herds of deer on the range, as they know they won't get shot. The bullets fly around 18ft over their heads (including the raised firing point) and none of us who compete want to waste money losing a competition shooting a deer. Anyway, definitely worth a look in.

This is an aspect of American gun crime which is not adequately acknowledged in most discussions of the subject because it's so deeply uncomfortable: Americans don't shoot each other at such high rates because they have guns, but (variously) because they are unsupervised, untrained, incorrectly trained, socially isolated, indoctrinated, psychologically unstable, intensely emotional, radicalized - to put it broadly, for cultural and personal reasons. In a general sense, Americans shoot each other more often because they are American. But having a conversation about why being American makes people more likely to want to shoot each other is much more difficult and fraught than having one about why guns make it easier for them to shoot each other, so people misdirect and focus on the access to guns instead of the motives. As if unhappy, unadjusted, violent, troubled people in a troubled society would suddenly become harmless if the easiest mode of violence were taken out of their reach.

Different country, slightly different regulation. In Czech Republic guns are treated quite same way like cars. You have to be healthy, learn how to handle gun (drive car), and prove it to state designated inspector together with quite tough test from knowledge of all regulations (traffic laws). There is one difference, that can forbid you from gun licence, but not driving one, criminal record.  All guns have to be registered (but not air guns, and low energy ones, which cannot kill or significantly harm people) and you cannot buy ammunition or gun without permit. On the other hand empty ammo is considered scrape metal and is treated as such. And I almost forgot, you can carry gun with you for sake of self defence.  We have some incidents with guns here in Czechia, but those are 99 percent hunt related. Unfortunately we had mass shooting here once, with have same amount of victims as another mass killing, done by .... car, which was chosen by murderer as easiest option how to kill big amount of people... and we are one of most peaceful countries in the word

This set up is actually inherently very dangerous and adds a lot of additional danger to a traditional shooting range set up. A lot of things could go wrong: 1) Someone stands up to shoot (actually a common shooting position in many ranges) and misses and hits a motorist below. Tragedy. 2) A stray bullet merely grazes the top of the wall and thus changes the path of a projectile towards a motorist. Tragedy. 3) A shooter uses subsonic ammunition such as .510 whisper, 300 blackout, .45 acp etc or any ammo that is slow enough to have a trajectory that can make it over the wall and then on to the motorists further down range and below. Tragedy. 4) A shooter uses a high calibre armour penetrating round that can make it though the concrete wall. Tragedy.
This is the kind attitude I wish we had in the U.S. firearms are as safe as the people using them. Unfortunately in the U.S too people buy guns without actually learning how to use them safely.

It's always good to point out that Switzerland has some of the toughest gun control in the world. I lived with a Swiss family, in which the father and two grown sons were soldiers - most Swiss men are - and therefore there were three large assault rifles in the house. The laws governing how those weapons are stored - they have to be broken down and the parts hidden from sight in three different places, which got challenging in a home that had to supply 9 hiding places) - and the ammunition for each, which must be stored in yet a fourth place, making 12 necessary storage places in all, are enforced by military officers who can and do conduct spot inspections of your home. And if you're found to have fewer shells in your possession than you were issued - big trouble.   As the fellow in the video mentioned, every firearm, military or civilian, also must be registered. And the sum total of other Swiss firearms laws is Byzantine and dizzying.  My hosts told me that in spite of the fact they're in almost every home, military firearms are very rarely used in crimes. Suicides were the main misuse.   Anyway, I find Switzerland often brought up in gun arguments. It's well to understand the full story.

WRONG! Switzerland has one of the highest rates of deaths per capita in the developed world despite not having any gang crime or violent urban centres etc. Why GUNS! The firearm-related death rate per 100,000 population per year in Switzerland is 2.64, in the UK it is 0.20. Take a look through gun deaths per 100,000 in Europe and Switzerland has one of, if not the highest, rates per capita and it has the most relaxed gun laws. So this proves that even in a nation with little poverty or other problems often blamed for high gun deaths, it is guns that lead to high gun deaths.    You can check all the countries that border Switzerland; Germany, France and even Italy all have lower per capita death rates from guns. In Switzerland you can go to the "Jungsch√ºtzen" which is the juniors (15-20y) section of those shooting ranges, for free! Teenagers learn how to shoot with the rifle you shot, stgw 90, over 300m. You also learn the most important thing, gun safety! It's very ingrained in our society, everyone I know that owns a gun has the necessary training and respect. Back when I was still a member of the shooting range, around 17 years old, I would have my personal assault rifle at home, but we would leave a piece (Verschluss idk the word in english) and the ammunition at the shooting range. I would walk through the main train station and would ride the train with the rifle on my back to go to the shooting range. No one ever bat an eye.

The best place to learn gun safety is going to a shooting range with an experience firearm owner. Even better is to take courses (whether required by state/federal law or not) and then go with an experience shooter. Learn the ins and outs of gun operation and range etiquette. I've seen rare instances of unsafe operation and those people are typically berated or even chased out of the range. Many of the most responsible and trustworthy people I know are gun owners and enthusiasts because responsible ownership requires discipline and attention to safety.

I grew up on a farm in New Zealand, and at the rear of our farm was a piece of land owned by the "Swiss Club" of Auckland (or maybe NZ), made up of ex-pat Swiss. They had cabins and playground stuff and it gave their members a nice place in the country to retreat to at the weekends and on holidays. They would host some awesome bbqs etc.  They also had a shooting range, and a few times a year they'd have at it.  I believe it was partly an enthusiast thing, and partly to do with keeping up their skills/training/requirements related to National Service. So even on the other side of the planet they love their firearms.  They were great people and many times you'd see dad driving down the hill on the tractor from helping them with fencing with a large, schnapps-infused grin on his face.

I used to go shooting with friend at a range in NJ.  They would cycle between firing periods and non-firing periods.  During the former, people were allowed to approach an available bench and set up their weapons.  Obviously, no one was allowed to point a weapon in any direction other than down range.  During the non-firing periods, everyone had to unload and safe their weapons, then step back from the bench.  Only when it was confirmed that everyone had done that were folks allowed on the range to check their targets.  No one was allowed to approach the benches during this time.  One time we were out on the range and heard a commotion back at the benches.  Someone had arrived and went to a bench to set up their guns and got permanently ejected as a result.

Tom, I'm a big fan of your videos (I'm an it-guy and linguist myself) and I'm also Swiss, and I think what you said in the last segment is wrong. Fear of your colleagues at the firing range is likely not a reason why nobody stands up and shoots at cars. Never say never.  I've lived in Switzerland for more than 25 years and in that time there were several incidents of people getting shot.  In one case a soldier who took his rifle home was found to have been shooting random people on the street.  In another someone entered the Zug cantonal government building and started shooting people.  In a third case someone went into a hotel near to my home and shot several people.  It is certainly very rare here but it does occur.  In general there is very little crime in Switzerland which I am convinced is because most Swiss live their whole lives in the region near to where they were born.  The country is small and there is no reason to move.  They form life long friendships and if they were ever to get caught committing a crime it would destroy their reputation for life.  I am originally from Canada where people move on average every five years so the social consequences of that are significantly lower as one can always move and start over.  I may be wrong but I think that is a big factor in why there is so little crime here and so much in North America.
As a Swiss I always enjoy videos like that. I'm also active in the swiss armed forces and I can confirm that we did get extensive training on firearmes. Most important we wehre also trained on how not to use them.  And trying to mess around at a shooting range would propably be as stupid as to rob a police station or entering a military base...  Since almost all gun owners are well trained we now how to use and store them save. Thats the reason you do not get headlines from Switzerland that somebody accidentaly shoot somebody.  The only shooting accident I encountered in my Military service was, when someone in my company accidentaly shoot a round in the disarming box while disarming the gun. I think for direct fire the range is safe.  The problem is that there is still a possibility that ricochet or shrapnel can harm or injure people within a certain distance from where the rifle is fired.  The probability is low, I'm sure, but it isn't zero.  If they increased the frequency of shooting from the range and increased the frequency of cars travelling across the path of flying projectiles, eventually it will reach a probability point where something happens.    For example, is it *impossible* that a bullet skimming the very top edge of the thick concrete wall could change trajectory in such a way that it strikes something a few degrees lower and a few degrees to the left or right of where it was originally heading?
